The Absurd Consequence Regarding Prophets and Iblis
The author shows the Mu'tazilite doctrine implies God should kill prophets and spare Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite principle of doing what is most beneficial leads to an absurd conclusion when applied to prophets and Iblis.

Core Idea
A false principle can force you to defend the absurd.

Sanusi continues his attack on the Mu'tazilite doctrine by showing that if God always does what is most beneficial, then killing prophets and sparing Iblis must be considered beneficial. This reductio ad absurdum exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: 'Then we also say to them... it follows for you that...'
2
Identify the Implied Principle
The Mu'tazilite principle being refuted is that God always does what is most beneficial (al-ashlah). See how the passage builds on that.
3
Understand the Reductio
The argument forces the opponent to accept that God's actual actions—killing prophets and leaving Iblis alive—must be the most beneficial. This is the absurd conclusion.
The phrase 'Kafā bi-ilzām hādhā shanā‘atan wa faḍāḥatan' shows the author's rhetorical punch.
4
Reflect on the Implication
Ask yourself: If the Mu'tazilite principle were true, would I have to accept that killing prophets is good? Why is this a scandal?
This exposes the inconsistency of the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم أيضاً : يلزمكم أن يكونَ ما فعله سبحانه ; مِنْ إماتة الأنبياء والرُّسُلِ الهادين المهتدين والعلماء العاملين والأولياء المرشدينَ ، وتبقية إبليس وذريَّتِهِ الضالين المضلين إلى يوم الدين. أصلح لعباده ، وكفى بإلزام هذا شناعة وفضاحة
Then we also say to them: 'It follows for you that what He, glorified, did, of causing the prophets and messengers, the guides and guided, the scholars who act, and the saints who guide to die, and leaving Iblis and his descendants, the stray and leading astray, until the Day of Judgment, must be more beneficial for His servants. And this implication is enough as a disgrace and scandal.'
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a further refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle by reductio ad absurdum. If God always does what is most beneficial, then His actions of causing the death of prophets and saints while leaving Iblis alive would have to be considered most beneficial, which is clearly absurd and scandalous.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite principle (al-ashlah) is obviously true and leads to reasonable conclusions.
Correction: The principle leads to the absurd conclusion that God killing prophets and sparing Iblis is most beneficial, which is a scandal.

Checkpoint

C) Iblis should be killed
D) Prophets should be left alive

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite principle of al-ashlah leads to the absurd conclusion that God's killing prophets and sparing Iblis is most beneficial, exposing its falsity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage ranks prophets and saints above Iblis in God's decree. Do you truly believe that God's wisdom in allowing evil is beyond your comprehension, or do you secretly expect it to make sense to you? What would it cost you to surrender that expectation?

Defining Prophethood and the Prophet-Messenger Distinction
Sanusi quotes a definition of prophethood and prophet, then notes an objection from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of prophethood and the distinction between a prophet and a messenger according to Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
A prophet is sent to convey revelation; a messenger is a prophet with a new law and book.

In this passage, Sanusi begins the chapter on prophecies by establishing foundational definitions. He quotes a classical definition that distinguishes prophets from messengers based on whether they bring a new law and scripture. However, a hadith objection complicates this distinction, which will be resolved later.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and distinction
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how prophethood is defined as being sent from God to creation, and then how a prophet is defined as a human sent to convey revelation. The messenger is similar but specified for those with a new law and book.
The passage distinguishes between a general prophet and a messenger who brings a new sharīʿa.
2
Identify the objection from hadith
After the definition, Sanusi mentions an objection: a hadith reports a higher number of messengers than prophets, which contradicts the idea that messengers are a subset. The answer is not given here.
This sets up a discussion that will be completed in later pages.
3
Consider the importance of the distinction
Reflect on why the distinction matters for understanding prophethood and the role of different prophets. It impacts how we view revelation, law, and the finality of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على النبوات باب كلام على ثبوت رسالة الرسل عليهم السلام عموماً وعلى ثبوت رسالة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم خصوصاً وبيان جنس دلالة المعجزة وتقريبها بالمثال تقدم الكلام على معنى النبوة والرسالة والفرق بينهما أول الكتاب في شرح الخطبة قال بعض الأئمة (2) : ( النبوة : هي كون الإنسان مبعوثاً من الحق إلى الخلق . والنبي : إنسان بعثه الله تعالى لتبليغ ما أوحى إليه , وكذلك الرسول , وقد يخص بمن له شريعة وكتاب , فيكون أخص من النبي . واعترض بما ورد في الحديث من زيادة عدد الرسل على عدد
Discourse on prophecies. Chapter. Discourse on the proof of the message of the messengers, upon them peace, in general, and on the proof of the message of our Prophet and master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, in particular, and an explanation of the kind of proof of the miracle and its illustration by example. The discourse on the meaning of prophethood and message and the difference between them has preceded at the beginning of the book in the commentary on the introduction. Some imams said (2): 'Prophethood is that a human is sent from the Truth to the creation. The prophet is a human whom God Almighty sent to convey what He revealed to him, and similarly the messenger, but it may be specified to one who has a law and a book, so it is more specific than the prophet.' And it was objected by what came in the hadith of the increase of the number of messengers over the number...
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on prophecies (al-nubuwwāt). It first states the chapter's purpose: to prove the mission of all messengers generally, and of Prophet Muhammad specifically, and to explain the evidential nature of miracles. Sanusi then notes that he has already discussed the meanings of prophethood (nubuwwa) and message (risāla) and their difference earlier in the book. He proceeds to quote a definition from a leading scholar (al-Taftazānī, as noted in the footnote): Prophethood is being sent by God to creation; a prophet is a human sent to convey revelation; a messenger is similar but is sometimes specified to one who brings a new law (sharīʿa) and a book, making 'messenger' more specific than 'prophet'. Immediately after this definition, Sanusi mentions an objection based on a hadith that reports a higher number of messengers than prophets. The objection is left incomplete at the page break, and the answer presumably follows in the next pages.

Messenger vs Prophet: The Defining Criteria
Sanusi defines and distinguishes rasūl and nabī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical distinction between a messenger (rasūl) and a prophet (nabī) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A messenger brings a book or abrogates laws; a prophet may do neither.

In Islamic theology, the terms 'messenger' and 'prophet' are often used interchangeably in casual speech, but scholars draw a precise distinction. This short passage from Sanusi lays out the core criterion: a messenger is defined by having a revealed book or abrogating previous laws, while a prophet may be free of both. Let's examine this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence, indicating it continues from a previous discussion.
The phrase 'so it was said then' shows this is a conclusion of a previous argument.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the messenger is defined (having a book or abrogation) and where the prophet is defined (may lack both). Note the example of Joshua.
3
Reflect on the relationship
Consider the logical relationship: every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. The passage implies this by giving Joshua as a prophet without messenger attributes.
This is a common classification in Islamic theology.
4
Test your understanding
Try to apply the criteria to other prophets mentioned in the Quran. For example, Prophet Muhammad had a book, so he is a messenger; Prophet Lot did not have a book of his own, so he is a prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتبِ ، فقيل حينئذٍ : الرسولُ مَنْ لهُ كتاب ، أو نسخ لبعض أحكام الشريعة السابقة ، والنبي قد يخلو عن ذلك ؛ كيوشع عليهِ الصلاة السلام ) (1) .
...so it was said then: The messenger is one who has a book, or abrogates some of the rulings of the previous law, and the prophet may be devoid of that, like Yushu‘ (Joshua), peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between a messenger (rasūl) and a prophet (nabī). The author states that a messenger is one who possesses a revealed book (kitāb) or abrogates certain rulings of a previous divine law (sharī‘a). A prophet, on the other hand, may lack both a book and the function of abrogation. The example given is Joshua (Yushu‘), who is considered a prophet but not a messenger having a book or abrogation. This distinction is a standard one in Islamic theology, used to differentiate the roles of prophets and messengers.

Key Terms
رسول
نبي
كتاب
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All prophets are messengers and all messengers are prophets.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a messenger is a subset: a messenger must have a book or abrogate, while a prophet may not. Thus, every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a messenger (rasūl) from a prophet (nabī)?
A) A messenger has a book or abrogates previous laws, while a prophet may lack both.
B) A messenger performs miracles, a prophet does not.
C) A prophet receives revelation, a messenger does not.
D) All messengers are prophets but not all prophets are messengers.

Key Takeaway
A messenger is a prophet who either brings a new book or abrogates previous laws, while a prophet may simply continue and teach the existing law without these functions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that a messenger brings a book or abrogates laws. Which of these two functions — bringing new guidance or correcting old practices — is missing in your community? Are you doing anything to fulfill it, or are you content to let others take that responsibility?
?
Joshua was a prophet without a book or abrogation. He faithfully implemented Moses' law. Are you faithful in implementing what you already know, or are you seeking novelty when you should first obey what is already commanded?
?
The definition of a messenger includes the possibility of abrogating previous rulings. When you enjoin good and forbid evil, do you have the authority to change practices? How does this lesson inform your humility as you rely on divine guidance rather than personal opinion?

Rational Permissibility of Sending Messengers
Sanusi proves rational permissibility and refutes two heresies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sending messengers is rationally permissible for God, and how this refutes the views of the Mu'tazila and the Brahmins.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is a permissible act of God, not an obligation.

This passage from Sanusi's work tackles a critical question: is God obligated to send messengers to humanity? Some groups said yes (Mu'tazila), others said no (Brahmins). Sanusi establishes the middle path: it is permissible, and He provides the rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the three parts: the definition of sending as a permissible act, the rational proof, and the refutation of two opposing sects.
The passage is dense but self-contained.
2
Understand the rational proof
Sanusi argues that sending is from God's actions, and no act is obligatory upon God. Therefore it is merely permissible, not obligatory or impossible. The great benefits do not create obligation.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the refutations
The Mu'tazila claimed sending is obligatory; the Brahmins claimed refraining is obligatory. Both are false. Sanusi declares the Brahmin view as clear disbelief.
Note the strong language against the Brahmins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعثةُ الرُّسُل جائزة في حقًّهِ تعالى ] ومِنَ الجائزاتِ : بعثه سبحانَهُ رُسُلَهُ للعبادِ ليبلغوهم أمرَ اللهِ تعالى ونهيه وإباحته وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ ، وأَيَّدَهم سبحانَهُ فضلاً منه بما يدلُّ على صِدْقِهم فيما بلغوا عنه ؛ بحيثُ يتنزَّلُ ذلكَ منزلة قولِهِ تعالى : صَدَقَ عبدي في كلِّ ما يُبلغ عني . أَمَّا كونُ بَعْثِ الرُّسُلِ جائزاً عقلاً : فلأنَّ البعثَ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللهِ تعالى ، وقد عرفتَ أنَّهُ لا يجب عليهِ جلَّ وعزّ فعل ، ولا يتحيَّمُ عليهِ تَرْكُ . وما في البعْثِ مِنْ عظيم المصالح للخلق لا يدلُّ على وجوبِهِ على الله تعالى ، كما تزعمه المعتزلة ؛ لما تقرَّرَ فيما سبقَ مِنْ عدمِ وجوبِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح للعباد على الله تعالى(۱) فبطل إذاً مذهبُ المعتزلة القائلين بوجوبِ بعْثِ اللهِ تعالى الرُّسُلَ الصلاة والسلام ، وبطل مذهب البراهمة القائلين بوجوبِ أنْ يترك الله بعْثَهم (۲) ، وأنَّهُ لا رسولَ لهُ أصلاً ، ومذهبهم كفر صُراح ، وهم أخس الناس أن يُتكلم معهم .
And from the permissible acts: His sending, may He be exalted, His messengers to the servants to convey to them the command of God, His prohibition, His permission, and what pertains to that. And He supported them, may He be exalted, as a favor from Him, with what indicates their truthfulness in what they conveyed from Him; to the extent that that takes the place of His statement: 'My servant has spoken truthfully in all that he conveys from Me.' As for the fact that sending messengers is rationally permissible: it is because sending is from the actions of God, and you have known that no act is obligatory upon Him, may He be glorified and exalted, nor is refraining necessarily incumbent upon Him. And what is in sending of great benefits for creation does not indicate its obligation upon God, as the Mu'tazila claim; because of what has been established previously of the non-obligation of considering what is beneficial and most beneficial for the servants upon God. So the school of the Mu'tazila who assert the obligation of God's sending messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, is invalidated. And the school of the Brahmins who assert the obligation of God's leaving their sending, and that there is no messenger for Him at all, is invalidated. And their school is clear disbelief, and they are the most contemptible of people to be spoken with.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage establishes the rational permissibility (jawāz) of God sending messengers, then refutes two opposing heresies. The author first states that sending messengers is among the permissible acts (jā’izāt) of God, for the purpose of conveying His commands, prohibitions, and permissions to servants, and He supports them with miracles that take the place of God's direct testimony. The rational proof: sending is an act of God, and it is established that no act is obligatory upon God nor is refraining incumbent upon Him. Therefore, sending is neither obligatory nor impossible—it is merely permissible. The benefits (maṣāliḥ) that follow from sending messengers do not make it obligatory, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who claimed God must do what is most beneficial. The author then declares the invalidity of both the Mu‘tazilī view (obligation to send) and the Brahmin view (obligation to refrain from sending, denying any messengers). The Brahmin position is described as clear disbelief (kufr ṣarīḥ) and their adherents as too contemptible to debate.

Key Terms
بعثة
جائز
وجوب
معتزلة
براهمة
مصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because sending messengers brings great benefits, it must be obligatory on God.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that God is not bound by considerations of benefit; no act is obligatory upon Him.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that sending messengers is not obligatory upon God?
A) Because God is not required to do anything for His creation.
B) Because no act is obligatory upon God and He is not forced to act or refrain.
C) Because the benefits of sending messengers are minimal.
D) Because messengers are not needed for guidance.

Key Takeaway
Sending messengers is a permissible act of God, and no benefit or lack thereof can make it obligatory or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes live as if God owes you guidance or ease because of your efforts? How does this lesson's assertion that sending messengers is a pure favor (not an obligation) challenge that assumption? What would change if you truly saw every prophet and every verse as an undeserved gift?
?
When you hear someone argue that God must act a certain way (e.g., must forgive, must reward), is there a part of you that agrees? This lesson declares that no act is obligatory upon God. Ask yourself: in what area of your life have you been expecting God to fulfill what you think He 'should' do? Will you repent of that presumption today?
?
The Brahmins' denial of all messengers is called 'clear disbelief.' But consider: do you ever treat your own intellect as sufficient, as if you don't need divine revelation? In what subtle ways might you be relying on your own reason over the messengers God has sent? What step will you take this week to submit more fully to what the messengers conveyed?

The Wisdom of Prophethood: Conveying Divine Rulings
Sanusi states the primary wisdom of sending messengers: to convey divine rulings to the responsible individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the primary wisdom behind sending messengers is to convey divine rulings (both prescriptive and declaratory) to responsible individuals.

Core Idea
The purpose of prophethood is to deliver God's rulings to humanity.

Sanusi begins by explaining the ultimate purpose of prophethood. Before discussing the details of messenger's attributes or proofs, he establishes that the very reason God sends messengers is to communicate His rulings. This passage lays the foundation for understanding why prophethood is necessary and what it achieves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how Sanusi directly states the wisdom (ḥikma) of sending messengers.
2
Identify the two types of rulings
The passage mentions prescriptive (taklīfiyya) and declaratory (waḍ‘iyya) rulings. Understand the distinction between them.
Prescriptive rulings include commands, prohibitions, and permissions; declaratory rulings include conditions and causes.
3
Analyze the quoted phrase
Sanusi quotes a previous phrase 'to convey to them the command of God, His prohibition, His permission, and what pertains to that' and explains that this points to the greatest benefit of prophethood.
The phrase summarizes the content of divine rulings.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why is conveying rulings considered the greatest wisdom? Consider that it guides human action and establishes accountability before God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمةُ مِنَ الرسالة تبليغ المكلَّفين الأحكام التكليفية والوضعية ] قوله : ( ليبلغوهم أمر الله تعالى ونهيه وإباحته وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ ) أشار بهذا إلى بعض الفوائدِ التي اشتملت عليها بعثةُ الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام فضلاً مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ، فذكر أعظمها وأشرفَها

His statement: 'to convey to them the command of God, His prohibition, His permission, and what pertains to that.' He indicated by this some of the benefits that the sending of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, comprised, as a favor from God. So he mentioned its greatest and most noble.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage explains the wisdom (ḥikma) behind the sending of messengers. The author states that the purpose is to convey to the legally responsible individuals (al-mukallafīn) both types of divine rulings: prescriptive rulings (aḥkām taklīfiyya, such as commands and prohibitions) and declaratory rulings (aḥkām waḍ‘iyya, such as conditions and causes). He then quotes the earlier phrase 'to convey to them the command of God, His prohibition, His permission, and what pertains to that' and notes that this points to some of the benefits of prophethood. The author emphasizes that among these benefits, the greatest and most noble is the communication of these rulings, as it is the primary function of the messengers.

Rational Permissibility of Sending Prophets
Sanusi proves the rational permissibility of prophethood and refutes opposing sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sending prophets is rationally permissible (ja'iz) according to Sanusi, and how this refutes both the Mu'tazilah who deem it obligatory and the Barahima who deem it impossible.

Core Idea
Sending prophets is rationally permissible—neither obligatory nor impossible.

Sanusi presents a concise proof that the sending of prophets is a rationally permissible act of God. He then demolishes two extreme positions: the Mu'tazilah who claim it is obligatory, and the Barahima who deny it entirely. This passage clarifies the foundational stance of Sunni theology on prophethood before addressing its necessity in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the rational ruling
Sanusi states that the ruling on sending prophets is ja'iz (permissible). Find his proof: sending is from the acts of God, and no act is obligatory upon Him.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Notice the two refutations
He refutes the Mu'tazilah who claim obligation due to benefits, and the Barahima who claim it must be avoided entirely. Understand why each is wrong based on the principle that God is not bound by created considerations.
4
Reflect on the core principle
The key takeaway is that God is not obliged to do what is beneficial for creation. This principle undercuts both extreme positions.
This connects to the broader theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا كونُ بَعْثِ الرُّسُلِ جائزاً عقلاً : فلأنَّ البعثَ مِنْ أَفعالِ اللهِ تعالى ، وقد عرفتَ أنَّهُ لا يجب عليهِ جلَّ وعزّ فعل ، ولا يتحيَّم[؟] عليهِ تَرْكُ . وما في البعْثِ مِنْ عظيم المصالح للخلق لا يدلُّ على وجوبِهِ على الله تعالى ، كما تزعمه المعتزلة ؛ لما تقرَّرَ فيما سبقَ مِنْ عدمِ وجوبِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح للعباد على الله تعالى فبطل إذاً مذهبُ المعتزلة القائلين بوجوبِ بعْثِ اللهِ تعالى الرُّسُلَ الصلاة والسلام ، وبطل مذهب البراهمة القائلين بوجوبِ أنْ يترك الله بعْثَهم ، وأنَّهُ لا رسولَ لهُ أصلاً ، ومذهبهم كفر صُراح ، وهم أخس الناس أن يُتكلم معهم .
As for the sending of messengers being rationally permissible: because sending is from the acts of God Most High, and you have known that no act is obligatory upon Him, may He be glorified and exalted, nor is its omission incumbent upon Him. And the great benefits in sending for creation do not indicate its obligation upon God Most High, as the Mu'tazilah claim; because it has been established previously that it is not obligatory upon God Most High to observe what is good and most suitable for the servants. Thus the doctrine of the Mu'tazilah who assert the obligation of God sending messengers (peace and blessings) is nullified, and the doctrine of the Barahima who assert that God must refrain from sending them, and that there is no messenger from Him at all, is nullified; and their doctrine is clear disbelief, and they are the lowest of people to be argued with.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Sanusi begins by stating the rational ruling on sending prophets: it is permissible (ja'iz). He provides the proof: sending is an act of God, and as established earlier, no act is obligatory upon God, nor is its omission incumbent. Therefore, it is not necessary (wajib) nor impossible (mumtani'), but possible. He then refutes two extreme positions. First, the Mu'tazilah who claim that sending prophets is obligatory upon God due to the benefits it brings. Sanusi rejects this by reiterating the principle that God is not obliged to consider what is beneficial for His creatures. Second, the Barahima who claim that God must not send prophets, and that there are no prophets at all. Sanusi declares their doctrine clear disbelief and dismisses them as unworthy of debate. He concludes by affirming the permissibility of prophethood against both extremes.

Key Terms
بعثة الرسل
جائز
واجب
معتزلة
براهمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because God is wise, He must send prophets; Sanusi clarifies that wisdom does not impose obligation on God.
Correction: The passage teaches that even great benefits do not obligate God; thus, sending prophets is not necessary but permissible.

Checkpoint
What is the rational ruling on sending prophets according to Sanusi?
A) Wajib (obligatory)
B) Ja'iz (permissible)
C) Mumtani' (impossible)
D) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
The sending of prophets is rationally permissible because God is not obligated to do any particular act, nor is He prevented from doing so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that Allah owes you something—health, wealth, or even guidance? How does this lesson's assertion that He is not obligated by any act challenge your sense of entitlement?

The Mu'tazilah assumed that because sending prophets brings benefits, it must be obligatory upon God. In what areas of your life do you assume that what seems good to you must be what Allah intends?
?
The Barahima denied all prophets, and Sanusi declares their position to be clear disbelief. Have you ever cherished doubts about the necessity of prophethood? If so, does this passage confront you with the seriousness of rejecting divine guidance?

Taklifi and Wad'i Rulings: The Purpose of Prophethood
Sanusi distinguishes between taklifi and wad'i rulings and explains their coverage in the creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between taklifi and wad'i rulings and how prophets convey these rulings to humanity.

Core Idea
The five taklifi rulings and three wad'i relations form the core of prophetic message.

Sanusi explains why God sends messengers: to convey two types of divine rulings. The first type are the five known as the taklifi rulings—obligation, prohibition, etc. The second type are the wad'i rulings, which establish causes, conditions, and preventives for the taklifi rulings. This passage lays the foundation for understanding Islamic jurisprudence from a theological perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines the two categories.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where Sanusi defines taklifi rulings (the five) and wad'i rulings (cause, condition, preventive). Write down the examples he gives.
The examples are key to understanding.
3
Understand the author's reference
Sanusi explains how the original creed's wording covers these rulings: 'His command' etc. Try to match each part of the phrase to the rulings.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Ask yourself why conveying these rulings is the primary purpose of prophethood. How does this relate to the definition of kalam?
This connects to the broader theme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمةُ مِنَ الرسالة تبليغ المكلَّفين الأحكام التكليفية والوضعية ] قوله : ( ليبلغوهم أمر الله تعالى ونهيه وإباحته وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ ) أشار بهذا إلى بعض الفوائدِ التي اشتملت عليها بعثةُ الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام فضلاً مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ، فذكر أعظمها وأشرفَها والمقصودَ الأَوَّلَ منها ؛ وذلك بيانُ أحكام الله تعالى التكليفيَّةِ والوضعيّة فالتكليفيةُ : هي الأحكامُ الخمسةُ ؛ الوجوب ، والتحريم ، والكراهة ، والندب ، والإباحة وأما الوضعيّةُ : فهي الحكم بسببيَّة أو شرطيَّةٍ أو مانعيّة لحُكْمِ مِنْ تلك الأحكام التكليفيَّة ؛ كالحكم على الزوالِ بأنَّه سبب لوجوبِ الظهر ، وعلى دخولِ شهرِ رمضانَ بأَنَّهُ سبب لوجوب الصوم ، وعلى الإسكارِ بأنَّهُ سبب لتحريم المسكر ، وكالحكم على مرورِ الحولِ بأنَّهُ شرط في زكاة بعض الأموال ، وكالحكم على الحيض بأنه مانع مِنْ وجوب الصلاة وصحة الصومِ . فأشار في أصلِ العقيدة إلى الأحكام التكليفيَّةِ بقوله : ( أمره ونهيه وإباحتَهُ ( لأنَّ مرادَهُ بالأمرِ : مطلق طلب الفعل ، فيدخل فيه الإيجاب والندب ، ويدخلُ في النهي التحريمُ والكراهة ، فهذه أربعة ، والخامس - وهو الإباحة - قد ذكرَهُ بلفظ يخصه . وأشار إلى الأحكام الوضعيّةِ بقولِهِ : ( وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ ) لأنَّ الأحكام الوضعية تتعلق بالتكليفية . ويدخلُ أيضاً في قوله : ( وما يتعلَّق بذلكَ ) ما بيَّنَهُ الشرعُ مِنَ الوعد والوعيد المترتبين على الامتثال وعدمِهِ ، وما شرحَهُ مِنْ أحوالِ
The wisdom of the message is to convey to the morally responsible persons the taklifi and wad'i rulings. His statement: 'to convey to them the command of God Most High, His prohibition, His permission, and what is related to that' — by this he alluded to some of the benefits that the sending of the messengers (peace and blessings) comprises, as a grace from God Most High. He mentioned the greatest and most noble of them and the primary purpose thereof: and that is the explanation of God Most High's taklifi and wad'i rulings. The taklifi rulings are the five rulings: obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, and permission. As for the wad'i rulings, they are the judgment of causality, conditionality, or preventiveness for a ruling from among those taklifi rulings; such as the judgment that the zenith is a cause for the obligation of the noon prayer, and that the entry of Ramadan is a cause for the obligation of fasting, and that intoxication is a cause for the prohibition of intoxicants; and such as the judgment that the passing of a year is a condition for the zakat of some properties, and the judgment that menstruation is a preventive from the obligation of prayer and the validity of fasting. So in the original creed he alluded to the taklifi rulings by his statement: 'His command, His prohibition, and His permission' because by 'command' he means absolute request for action, which includes obligation and recommendation; and in 'prohibition' enters prohibition and dislike; thus these are four, and the fifth — which is permission — he mentioned with a specific term. And he alluded to the wad'i rulings by his statement: 'and what is related to that' because the wad'i rulings are related to the taklifi rulings. Also included in his statement 'and what is related to that' is what the Law has clarified of promise and threat that result from compliance or non-compliance, and what He has explained of the states...
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the rulings that prophets convey. Sanusi first states the purpose of the prophetic message: to inform the mukallafun (morally responsible) of two types of rulings: taklifiyyah and wad'iyyah. He then quotes the text from the original creed (which he is commenting on) that mentions 'His command, His prohibition, His permission, and what is related to that.' He notes that the author of the original text pointed to the greatest benefit: explaining these rulings. Sanusi then defines each type. Taklifiyyah are the five rulings: wujub (obligation), tahrim (prohibition), karaha (dislike), nadb (recommendation), and ibaha (permission). Wad'iyyah are rulings that establish something as a cause (sabab), condition (shart), or preventive (mani') for a taklifi ruling, with examples: the zenith as cause for obligatory noon prayer, entry of Ramadan as cause for obligatory fasting, intoxication as cause for prohibition of intoxicants; the passing of a year as condition for zakat; menstruation as preventive from prayer obligation and fast validity. Sanusi then explains how the original text's wording covers these: 'His command' encompasses obligation and recommendation, 'His prohibition' encompasses prohibition and dislike, and 'His permission' is the fifth; 'and what is related to that' covers wad'i rulings because they relate to taklifi rulings, and also includes promise and threat.

Key Terms
أحكام تكليفية
أحكام وضعية
سبب
شرط
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five rulings are sometimes confused between the two categories; some think the five include wad'i rulings.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that taklifi rulings are the five (obligation, prohibition, dislike, recommendation, permission), while wad'i rulings are causes, conditions, and preventives that relate to the taklifi rulings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a wad'i (relational) ruling?
A) Obligation of prayer
B) The entry of Ramadan is a cause for obligation of fasting
C) Prohibition of alcohol
D) Recommendation of charity

Key Takeaway
The purpose of prophethood is to explain both the five taklifi rulings and the relational wad'i rulings that establish causes, conditions, and preventives for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a prayer, do you consciously connect the physical cause (the time) as part of your obedience to Allah, or do you merely go through the motion? This lesson says the purpose of prophethood is to teach both the command and its causes—how does this change the way you experience your daily worship?
?
Think of a time you neglected a condition for an act of worship (like purity, or the year for zakat). Did you realize you were dismissing not just a detail, but a core part of the prophetic message? What will you do differently now to honor both the command and its conditions?
?
The threat of punishment and promise of reward are included in 'what is related to that.' Does your fear of Hell actually drive you to examine the causes and preventives of your actions, or do you only focus on avoiding obvious sins? How can you let this lesson reshape your spiritual accountability starting today?

Divine Support for Prophets: Signs of Truthfulness
Commentary on 'وأيدهم سبحانه فضلاً منه' and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah provides prophets with clear signs of truthfulness and who is capable of doubting them.

Core Idea
Only those decreed to punishment will doubt a prophet's clear signs.

Prophethood is a claim that could be made by both truthful and false individuals. To protect His message, Allah grants true prophets unmistakable signs of their truthfulness. This lesson explores the wisdom behind these divine signs and the condition of those who still reject them.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the original Arabic and its English translation to get a direct sense of the author's argument.
2
Identify the problem addressed
Notice that the passage starts by stating that the claim of prophethood can come from both the truthful and the liar. This sets up the need for distinguishing signs.
3
Understand the divine favor
Reflect on the statement that Allah supports the truthful with clear indications of his truthfulness as a favor. Ask: Why is this called a favor?
It is a favor because it removes excuse and makes the truth evident.
4
Examine the exception
Consider the phrase 'except one upon whom punishment has been decreed' and understand that rejecting clear signs is a result of divine abandonment.
This highlights the gravity of denying a prophet after clear signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأيَّدَهُم سبحانَهُ فضلاً منه بما يدلُّ على صِدْقِهم ) يعني : دعوى النبوَّةِ لمَّا كانَتْ تقعُ مِنَ الصادقِ والكاذب . . تفضَّلَ مولانا جل وعز بعظيم كرمِهِ وسَعَةِ فَضْلِهِ بأنْ أيَّد الصادق بما يدلُّ على صدقِهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يستريبُ معَ ذلكَ فِي صِدْقِهِ إِلا مَنْ حَقَّتْ العذاب ، وابتُلي بالخِذْلانِ والطردِ عن كلَّ خيرٍ ، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله .
His statement: 'And He, glorified, supported them as a favor from Him with what indicates their truthfulness' means: The claim of prophethood, since it can come from both the truthful and the liar... He, our Master, majestic and mighty, bestowed His immense generosity and abundant favor by supporting the truthful with what indicates his truthfulness; such that no one doubts his truthfulness except one upon whom punishment has been decreed, and who is afflicted with abandonment and expulsion from all good. There is no power nor strength except with Allah.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a phrase from the text being commented on. It begins by noting that the claim of prophethood can be made by both truthful and false individuals. Then it explains that Allah, in His generosity, supports the truthful with clear signs that prove his truthfulness. The verse clarifies that only those who are doomed to punishment and have been abandoned by divine grace will doubt such clear signs. The passage ends with a formula of reliance on Allah.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the signs are so clear that only those upon whom punishment has been decreed will doubt them. Doubt after clear signs is a sign of divine abandonment.

B) The liar
C) Only those upon whom punishment is decreed
D) All people

Key Takeaway
Allah supports true prophets with unmistakable signs, and only those abandoned by divine grace reject them.

?

?
When you hear about the clear signs of prophethood, does your soul submit immediately, or is there hesitation? What is the root of that hesitation, and are you willing to ask Allah to remove it?

The Messenger's Truthfulness: God's Knowledge and Personal Integrity
The prophet argues that he cannot lie because God sees all and his character is truthful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prophet's truthfulness is supported by God's omniscience and the prophet's own impeccable character.

Core Idea
A true messenger cannot lie to an all-knowing God, and his life proves it.

This passage presents the prophet's own argument for his truthfulness. He first establishes that the King (God) is fully aware of his words and actions, making any lie impossible to hide. Then he appeals to his lifelong honesty, even among ordinary people, to show that lying about God would be even more unthinkable. Let's examine this dual foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical questions and emotional tone.
2
Identify God's role in the argument
The prophet emphasizes that God is all-seeing and all-hearing, and that He has the power to punish immediately. This makes it impossible for the prophet to lie safely.
3
Consider the prophet's character
The prophet points to his lifelong honesty, even in matters that would not bring divine punishment. He has never lied to anyone, so how could he lie about God?
4
Synthesize the two arguments
Reflect on how the external (God's knowledge) and internal (prophet's character) proofs work together to establish his truthfulness.
This is the unique contribution of this passage.

وقد أنهيتُ إليكم رسالة الملكِ ؛ فمَنْ أطاعَهُ ، وأحسنَ النظر لنفسِهِ . . فقد استخلصها ، واغتنم عظيمَ رضاه ، ومَنْ عصاه ، وأهملَ النظر لنفسِهِ . . فقد تعرَّضَ لما لا يُطاقُ مِنْ هول سخط الملكِ ، ولا أحد يطيقُ إنقاذهُ مِنْ عظيم رَدَاهُ وقولي هذا تعلمون أنَّهُ بِعِلْمٍ مِنَ الملكِ ومرأى منهُ الآنَ ومَسْمَع ، وإِنَّهُ وإِنْ حَجَبَنا الآن عن مشاهدته فليس هو محجوباً عن رؤيتنا وسماع ما يجري بيننا ، وهو الذي يضعُ مَنْ يشاءُ ويرفعُ ، وهو القادرُ أنْ يعاقبني إِنْ كذبتُ عنه ولا ملجأ لي إن عصيته ولا مهرب لي ولا مدفع وقد عهدتُموني مِنْ لدن نشأتي : لا أسمح لنفسي بكذبة على مَنْ هو مثلي وعلى شاكلتي ، وإنْ نفعَتْني وأمنَتْ فيها من كل ضرر ناحيتي ، فكيف أتجاسر بعد ما تكامل عقلي ونقصت صبوتي ، واشتعل الشيب في صدغي ولحيتي . . على أن أكذب على الملكِ بمرأى منهُ ومَسْمَع ، مع علمي بعظيم سطوته وقهره ، وأليم عقوبتِهِ لمَن تعرَّض لجانبه العلي واستخف بعظيم أمره ؟! فأي سماء تظلُّني ، وأي أرض تقلني .. إن كذبتُ عنه حرفاً ؟! وأنا أتحقق أني لو تقوَّلْتُ عليهِ بعض الأقاويل ، وفُهْتُ لكم عنهُ خُلْفاً . . لأخذ مني باليمين ، ولقطع مني الوتين ، ولا أجد منكم أحداً عني حاجزين . ثم إن لم يقنعكم هذا في تحققِ صِدْقِ مقالتي ، واستربتُم فيَّ معَ ما جرَّبْتُمُ التجريب التام مِنْ كمال نصحي لكم ، وشدَّةِ رأفتي بكم ، وعظيم شفقتي ، وشَرَفِ سابقتي ، وتنزهي عن كل رذيلة ، خصوصاً رذيلة الكذب وما تتحققونَ مِنْ حُسْنِ سيرتي .. فهنا ما يقطعُ العذر لكلِّ أحدٍ ، وتطلعُ به شموس المعرفة الضرورية على آفاقِ القلوب حتى لا ينكرها إلا مَنْ تعرّض لسخط الملكِ ، وحقَّتْ عليه كلمةُ العذابِ فعاندَ وجَحَدَ ؛ وذلكَ أَنْ أَسأَلَ الملك كما تفضَّلَ ببعثي إليكم لبيان مراشدكم وإنذاركم قبل ما يفوتُ معَهُ استعدادكم لمعادِكم .. يتفضَّلُ أيضاً بإبانة صدقي فيما عنهُ بلَّغْتُ ، وأنّي ما كذبْتُ عنه
And I have completed to you the message of the King. So whoever obeys Him and looks well to himself has attained it and gained His great pleasure. And whoever disobeys Him and neglects looking to himself has exposed himself to unbearable horror of the King's wrath, and no one can rescue him from great destruction. And this my statement, you know that it is with knowledge of the King and sight of Him now and hearing. And though He has veiled us from seeing Him now, He is not veiled from seeing us and hearing what transpires among us. And He is the One who abases whomever He wills and exalts, and He is able to punish me if I lie about Him, and there is no refuge for me if I disobey Him, nor any escape or defense. And you have known me since my upbringing: I do not allow myself a lie against someone like me and of my kind, even if it benefits me and I am safe from any harm from my side. So how could I dare, after my intellect has matured, my youth has diminished, and gray hair has appeared in my temples and beard, to lie about the King while He sees and hears, with my knowledge of His great might and force, and the painful punishment for whoever exposes himself to His high side and scoffs at His great command? So what sky would shelter me, and what earth would carry me, if I lied about Him a single letter? And I am certain that if I invented sayings about Him and uttered to you falsehood from Him, He would seize me by the right hand and cut my aorta, and I would not find among you anyone to shield me. Then if this does not convince you of the truth of my statement, and you doubt me with what you have fully experienced of the perfection of my advice to you, the intensity of my compassion for you, the greatness of my mercy, the honor of my past, my freedom from every vice, especially the vice of lying, and what you verify of the goodness of my conduct—then here is that which cuts off excuse for everyone, and by which the suns of necessary knowledge rise upon the horizons of hearts so that none denies them except one who exposes himself to the King's wrath and the word of punishment is justified against him, so he obstinately resists and denies. And that is that I ask the King, just as He favored by sending me to you to clarify your guides and warn you before your preparation for your return passes away, that He also favor by making clear my truthfulness in what I have conveyed from Him, and that I have not lied about Him.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical proof for the truthfulness of a messenger (a prophet or envoy). The speaker, who claims to have delivered the King's message, begins by stating the stakes: obedience brings reward, disobedience brings destruction. He then emphasizes that the King is omniscient and omnipresent, aware of all actions and speech, even when veiled from sight. The King has absolute power to punish, so lying against Him would be futile and dangerous. Next, the speaker appeals to his own character: he has never lied to anyone, even when it was safe to do so, and now that he is mature and wise, it would be absurd to lie about the all-seeing, all-powerful King. He stresses that lying would lead to immediate divine punishment (seizing by the right hand, cutting the aorta) with no protector. Finally, if the audience still doubts, the speaker offers a decisive proof: he will ask the King to grant a clear sign (a miracle) to confirm his truthfulness. This sign is described as 'necessary knowledge' that dawns on hearts, leaving no excuse for denial except obstinacy. Thus, the passage presents three lines of evidence for the messenger's truthfulness: the impossibility of deceiving an all-knowing God, the messenger's impeccable character, and the willingness to produce a supernatural sign. This is a classic kalam argument for the veracity of prophets (sidq al-anbiya').

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet's truthfulness is known only through miracles.
Correction: This passage shows that reason (God's omniscience) and character also provide proof.

Key Takeaway
The prophet's truthfulness is supported both by the impossibility of deceiving an all-knowing God and by his consistent honest character.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The prophet's consistency from youth to old age is used as evidence of his truthfulness. Are you the same person in private as in public? What one habit of dishonesty—even towards yourself—are you willing to abandon right now after reading this lesson?

The Decisive Proof: Requesting a Miracle
The prophet offers to request a miracle as the final proof of his truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ultimate proof of a prophet's truthfulness: the request for a supernatural sign.

Core Idea
If reason and character are not enough, a miracle from God settles the matter.

After presenting his character and God's knowledge, the prophet addresses persistent doubters. He offers to ask God for a clear sign that will leave no excuse for denial. This sign is described as necessary knowledge that dawns on hearts. This final proof is the cornerstone of prophetic verification in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the shift from personal appeal to offering a sign.
The prophet is speaking to those who might still doubt despite his character.
2
Understand the offer
The prophet proposes to ask God for a sign to confirm his truthfulness. This is not a demand but a request for divine confirmation.
3
Grasp the nature of the sign
The sign provides 'necessary knowledge' (marifa daruriyya) that dawns on hearts. It is so clear that only obstinate deniers would reject it.
This is a key term in kalam.
4
Consider the consequences
Those who still deny after such a sign do so out of obstinacy and deserve the King's wrath.

Prophethood

Miracles as Divine Confirmation of Prophetic Truthfulness
Sanusi presents a rhetorical scenario to demonstrate the logic of miracles as divine confirmation of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles function as divine testimony to a prophet's truthfulness, producing necessary knowledge (al-ma'rifah al-ḍarūriyyah) for those who witness or reliably hear of them.

Core Idea
A miracle is God's act of breaking custom to confirm a prophet's truthfulness.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a vivid rhetorical scenario to illustrate the logic of prophetic miracles. A speaker, claiming to be a messenger of God, first emphasizes his sincerity through an oath and then offers a definitive proof: he will ask God to perform a miracle that only He can do, and God will grant it. This miracle becomes a divine declaration of the speaker's truthfulness, accessible as necessary knowledge to those present or those who receive the report via tawātur. Sanusi then explicitly applies this to the case of all prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with attention to the rhetorical flow
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the speaker builds his case from an oath of sincerity to the final proof of a miracle. Let the dramatic language set the stage for the theological argument.
The passage is written in a first-person narrative, as if the speaker is addressing his audience directly.
2
Identify the two levels of proof
The speaker first appeals to his character and the fear of divine punishment if he were lying. Then, in case of continued doubt, he proposes a supernatural sign. Distinguish these two forms of evidence.
The second proof is decisive and cuts off all excuses.
3
Analyze the nature of the miracle
The speaker asks God to break His custom (kharq al-'ādah) by doing something He does not normally do. The miracle is specific to the truthful claimant and cannot be replicated by trickery. Understand why this makes it a divine confirmation.
The act is described as 'that distinguishing confirmation' (al-muṣaddiq al-fāriq).
4
Grasp the conclusion about knowledge
The passage concludes that such a miracle takes the place of God explicitly declaring the person's truthfulness, and that knowledge of this is necessary (ḍarūrī) for witnesses or those who receive reliable reports. Apply this to the prophets.
This connects to the concept of tawātur (widespread transmission) and necessary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعظيم سطوته وقهره ، وأليم عقوبتِهِ لمَن تعرَّض لجانبه العلي واستخف بعظيم أمره ؟! فأي سماء تظلُّني ، وأي أرض تقلني .. إن كذبتُ عنه حرفاً ؟! وأنا أتحقق أني لو تقوَّلْتُ عليهِ بعض الأقاويل ، وفُهْتُ لكم عنهُ خُلْفاً . . لأخذ مني باليمين ، ولقطع مني الوتين ، ولا أجد منكم أحداً عني حاجزين . ثم إن لم يقنعكم هذا في تحققِ صِدْقِ مقالتي ، واستربتُم فيَّ معَ ما جرَّبْتُمُ التجريب التام مِنْ كمال نصحي لكم ، وشدَّةِ رأفتي بكم ، وعظيم شفقتي ، وشَرَفِ سابقتي ، وتنزهي عن كل رذيلة ، خصوصاً رذيلة الكذب وما تتحققونَ مِنْ حُسْنِ سيرتي .. فهنا ما يقطعُ العذر لكلِّ أحدٍ ، وتطلعُ به شموس المعرفة الضرورية على آفاقِ القلوب حتى لا ينكرها إلا مَنْ تعرّض لسخط الملكِ ، وحقَّتْ عليه كلمةُ العذابِ فعاندَ وجَحَدَ ؛ وذلكَ أَنْ أَسأَلَ الملك كما تفضَّلَ ببعثي إليكم لبيان مراشدكم وإنذاركم قبل ما يفوتُ معَهُ استعدادكم لمعادِكم .. يتفضَّلُ أيضاً بإبانة صدقي فيما عنهُ بلَّغْتُ ، وأنّي ما كذبْتُ عنه وما نَزَغْتُ ؛ بأن يخرق عادتهُ ، ويفعل كذا وكذا ممَّا ليسَ عادته أنْ يفعله ، ويخصني بالإجابة بذلك المصدقِ الفارقِ ، دونَ مَنْ يقومُ منكم يسأله مثل ذلكَ الخارقِ ، يبتغي به معارضتي ، وتكذيبي في مقالتي ؛ إذ ليس هو في الصِّدْقِ على مثل حالتي ثم قالَ : أَيُّها الملكُ ؛ إنْ كنتُ صادقاً فيما بلَّغْتُ عنكَ . . فاخرق عادتكَ ، وافعل كذا فأجابه الملكُ إلى ذلكَ ، وفعلَهُ على وَفْقِ ما سأَلَ وقد علم الجميعُ أَنَّهُ لا يُتوصَّلُ إلى مثل ذلكَ الفعلِ مِنَ الملكِ بحيلةٍ مِنَ الحيل . · فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ ذلكَ الفعلَ مِنَ الملكِ يتنزَّلُ منزلة تصريحه بصِدْقِ الشخص في كلّ ما يُبلغ عنه ، والعلم بذلك ضروري لمَنْ حضرَ ذلكَ المجلس أو غابَ عنهُ ووصله خبره بالتواتر . ولا يخفى : أنَّ هذا المثالَ مطابق لحالِ الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ قد عُلِمَ ضرورةً مِنْ سيرتهم
[The speaker says:] By the greatness of His power and His overwhelming might, and the painful punishment for whoever confronts His high station and treats lightly His great command?! So which sky would shelter me, and which earth would carry me, if I lied about Him even a letter?! And I am certain that if I fabricated some sayings against Him, and uttered to you something contrary from Him, He would seize me by the right hand, and cut my aorta, and you would not find anyone to shield me from you. Then if this does not convince you of the truth of my statement, and you still doubt me despite your complete experience of the perfection of my advice to you, and the intensity of my compassion for you, and the greatness of my affection, and the honor of my precedence, and my being free from every vice, especially the vice of lying, and what you verify from the goodness of my conduct—then here is something that cuts off every excuse, and the suns of necessary knowledge rise upon the horizons of hearts so that none denies it except one who exposes himself to the wrath of the King, and the word of punishment becomes due upon him, so he obstinately persists and denies; and that is that I ask the King, just as He graciously sent me to you to clarify your guidance and warn you before your preparation for your return becomes impossible, to also graciously manifest my truthfulness regarding what I have conveyed from Him, and that I have not lied about Him nor slandered Him, by breaking His custom and doing such-and-such which is not His custom to do, and to single me out with a response by that distinguishing confirmation, apart from anyone among you who stands and asks Him for a similar miraculous act seeking to oppose me and deny my statement, for he is not in truthfulness in a similar state to mine. Then he said: O King, if I am truthful in what I have conveyed about You, then break Your custom and do such-and-such. And the King granted him that, and did it according to what he asked. And everyone knows that such an act from the King cannot be achieved through any trick. Therefore, there is no doubt that that act from the King takes the place of His declaration of the person's truthfulness in everything he conveys about Him, and knowledge of that is necessary for whoever attended that assembly or was absent from it and received its report by widespread transmission. And it is not hidden that this example corresponds to the case of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, and there is no doubt that it is known necessarily from their biographies.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration of the argument that miracles (kharq al-'ādah) serve as divine confirmation of a prophet's truthfulness. The author presents a rhetorical scenario in the first person, where a speaker addresses his audience with a powerful oath: if he were lying about God, he would face immediate divine punishment—God would seize him and cut his aorta, and no one could protect him. This establishes the speaker's fear and sincerity. However, if the audience still doubts, the speaker proposes an ultimate proof: he will ask God to break His custom (perform a miracle) specifically in response to his request, and God will grant it. The act is described as something not normally done by God, and it is known that no trickery can produce it. This miraculous act then serves as God's declaration of the speaker's truthfulness in all he conveys. The knowledge of this is necessary (ḍarūrī) for those present or those who receive the report via tawātur (mass transmission). Finally, the author explicitly applies this to the prophets (peace be upon them), stating that this example matches their situation and that this fact is known necessarily from their biographies.

Key Terms
الخارق
التواتر
الصدق
المعرفة الضرورية
خرق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that miracles are merely coincidental or could be produced by human trickery.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that such miracles are known to be beyond any trick (ḥīlah), and they are specific to the truthful claimant, serving as a divine confirmation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the miracle requested from the King accomplish?
A) It proves that the King exists.
B) It takes the place of the King's declaration of the person's truthfulness in everything he conveys about Him.
C) It shows that the person has magical powers.
D) It forces everyone to believe without choice.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a divine act that breaks natural custom solely to confirm a prophet's truthfulness, producing necessary knowledge for its witnesses and for those who receive credible reports of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophet in the scenario says: 'If I lied about Him, He would seize me and cut my aorta.' Do you speak about God—your beliefs, practices, and teachings—with the same absolute fear of misrepresenting Him? Is there any area of your life where you stretch the truth about your faith for convenience? What would it cost you to be completely honest about your commitment?
?
God's miracle 'cuts off every excuse' for those who hear of it. Think about the truth of Islam that has reached you through clear signs. What excuse—big or small—are you still holding onto that prevents you from fully living by that truth? Will you let it go today, knowing that holding it is like exposing yourself to the King's wrath?
?
The miracle is described as a 'distinguishing confirmation' that singles out the truthful. If you were to be tested by a sign from God today, would your inner state—your sincerity, your freedom from hidden vices—match the prophet's? What one hidden vice, if any, do you need to confess and abandon so that your life might be a true reflection of the message you claim to believe? Do not delay.

Prophethood

The King's Miracle Analogy
Sanusi presents an analogy for miracles as proof of truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a king's extraordinary act demonstrates that a prophet's miracle functions as God's explicit declaration of his truthfulness.

Core Idea
A miracle is God's explicit declaration that the prophet is truthful.

Sanusi begins with a vivid analogy: a person claiming to speak for a king requests the king to perform an extraordinary act. When the king complies in a way no trickery can achieve, that act becomes the equivalent of an explicit statement that the claimant is truthful. This analogy lays the foundation for understanding how miracles serve as divine confirmation of prophethood, accessible even to those who only hear about them through reliable transmission (tawātur).

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the Arabic passage describing the king scenario. Notice the structure: the claimant asks for a miracle and the king complies.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the claimant, the king, the extraordinary act (khāriq al-ʿāda), and the audience. See how the act functions as a declaration.
The act is beyond trickery, making it a true sign.
3
Connect to prophecy
Understand that miracles serve the same role for prophets as the king's act did for the claimant: direct divine confirmation of their truthfulness.
The passage explicitly states the analogy applies to messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نَزَغْتُ ؟ بأن يخرق عادتهُ ، ويفعل كذا وكذا ممَّا ليسَ عادته أنْ يفعله ، ويخصني بالإجابة بذلك المصدقِ الفارقِ ، دونَ مَنْ يقومُ منكم يسأله مثل ذلكَ الخارقِ ، يبتغي به معارضتي ، وتكذيبي في مقالتي ؛ إذ ليس هو في الصِّدْقِ على مثل حالتي ۱ ثم قالَ : أَيُّها الملكُ ؛ إنْ كنتُ صادقاً فيما بلَّغْتُ عنكَ . . فاخرق عادتكَ ، وافعل كذا فأجابه الملكُ إلى ذلكَ ، وفعلَهُ على وَفْقِ ما سأَلَ وقد علم الجميعُ أَنَّهُ لا يُتوصَّلُ إلى مثل ذلكَ الفعلِ مِنَ الملكِ بحيلةٍ مِنَ الحيل . فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ ذلكَ الفعلَ مِنَ الملكِ يتنزَّلُ منزلة تصريحه بصِدْقِ الشخص في كلّ ما يُبلغ عنه ، والعلم بذلك ضروري لمَنْ حضرَ ذلكَ المجلس أو غابَ عنهُ ووصله خبره بالتواتر . ولا يخفى : أنَّ هذا المثالَ مطابق لحالِ الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ قد عُلِمَ ضرورةً مِنْ سيرتهم عليهم الصلاةُ السلامُ التزامُ الصِّدْقِ ، ورفع الهمَّةِ عن كل دناءة ، والزهد في الدنيا بأسرها ؛ بحيث استوى عندهم ذهبُها ومدرُها ، والتزامُ غـايـة الـتـواضـع مـعَ الفقراء والمساكين ، وإسقاط الجاه والمنزلة عندَ الخَلْقِ ، وطلبها عند الملكِ الحقِّ ، وعظيم ما جُبلوا عليهِ مِنَ الشفقة على المخلوقين ، والنصحُ التام لعبادِ اللهِ تعالى ، وكثرةُ جميع الخوفِ منه جلَّ وعزَّ ، والمبادرة لامتثالِ ما بَلَّغوا عنه قبل كلّ أحدٍ ، والمواظبة إلى المماتِ على دعاءِ الخَلْقِ إِلى اللهِ تعالى ، مع التسوية في ذلكَ بين وضيعهم ورفيعهم ، وغنيهم وفقيرهم ، وفطينهم وبليدهم ، وأعجمهم وفصيحهم، وحُرِّهم وعبدِهم، وذكرهم وأنثاهم . وحاضرهم وغائبهم ، وملكهم وسوقتِهم ثم سعة الصدور لحمل سوء إيذائهم وشدَّةِ جفائهم والرأفة على جميعِهم أكثرَ مِنْ رأفتِهم على أولادهم بل وعلى أنفسهم ، مِنْ غيرِ عوض يأخذونه منهم ، ولا منفعة دنيوية تحصل لهم مِنْ قِبَلِهم ، بل هم عليهم الصلاة والسلام تعرَّضوا بذلك لشدائد وأهوال نالتهم مِنْ جهتِهم ، لا يثبتُ لها إلا مَنْ هو على صميم الحقِّ ، قد شغلَهُ التلذذ برضا
And I did not [؟] that He should break His habit, and do such-and-such which is not His habit to do, and single me out for a response with that distinguishing sign, apart from whoever among you stands and asks Him for a similar miracle, seeking thereby to oppose me and deny my claim; for he is not in truthfulness in my state. Then he said: O king, if I am truthful in what I have conveyed from you, then break your habit and do such-and-such. So the king answered him and did it in accordance with what he asked. And everyone knew that such an action from the king cannot be achieved by any trick. So it is no secret that that action from the king takes the place of his explicit declaration of the person's truthfulness in everything he reports about him, and knowledge of that is necessary for whoever attended that assembly or was absent from it and whose report reached him via tawātur. Nor is it hidden that this example corresponds to the situation of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, and it is no secret that it is known necessarily from their biography, peace and blessings be upon them: their commitment to truthfulness, the elevation of their aspiration above every baseness, and their renunciation of the whole world such that its gold and dust were equal to them; their commitment to utmost humility with the poor and needy; their disregard for prestige and status among creatures, seeking them only with the true King; the great compassion with which they were disposed towards creatures; their sincere counsel to the servants of God Most High; their abundant fear of Him, Mighty and Majestic; their hastening to obey what they conveyed from Him before anyone else; their perseverance until death in calling creatures to God Most High, treating equally in that the low and the high, the rich and the poor, the intelligent and the dull, the non-Arab and the eloquent, the free and the slave, the male and the female, the present and the absent, the king and the commoner; then their forbearance in bearing the harm of their mistreatment and the severity of their cruelty; their compassion for all of them more than their compassion for their own children, even more than for themselves, without any recompense they take from them or any worldly benefit they obtain from them; rather, they, peace and blessings be upon them, exposed themselves thereby to hardships and terrors that befell them from them, which none endures except one who is upon solid truth, who is occupied by the delight of [God's] pleasure...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents a twofold proof of the truthfulness of prophets. First, it develops an analogy: a person claiming to speak on behalf of a king requests the king to perform an extraordinary act (khāriq al-ʿāda) as a sign of his truthfulness. The king complies in a way that no trickery can achieve. This act is equivalent to the king's explicit declaration that the person is truthful in all he reports. Knowledge of this is necessary (ḍarūrī) for those who witness it or receive tawātur reports. The analogy is then applied to the prophets: their miracles (muʿjizāt) serve the same function as the king's extraordinary act, providing direct divine confirmation of their truth. Second, the passage argues that the known character of the prophets—their truthfulness, disdain for worldly gain, humility, compassion, fear of God, perseverance in calling people to God despite harm, and their impartiality towards all people—is so impeccable that it necessarily (bi-ḍarūra) establishes their truthfulness. This character is known from their widely transmitted (mutawātir) biographies. The passage emphasizes that the prophets endured severe hardships without seeking any worldly benefit, proving their sincerity. The combination of miracles and moral perfection leaves no doubt about their prophethood.

Key Terms
خارق العادة
تصريح
ضرورة
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any extraordinary event can prove prophethood, even if it could be achieved by trickery.
Correction: The analogy emphasizes that the act must be beyond any possible trick, ensuring it is a true miracle that can only come from God.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, what does the king's extraordinary act represent?
A) A fleeting wonder with no consequence.
B) An explicit declaration that the claimant is truthful.
C) A test of the claimant's sincerity.
D) A reward for the claimant's loyalty.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is God's unambiguous declaration that a prophet is truthful, known with certainty by witnesses and through reliable transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a miracle is God’s explicit declaration that a prophet is truthful, then what does your hesitation to fully submit to the Prophet’s message (peace be upon him) say about your trust in God’s own signature? How many more signs do you need before you live as if His Messenger is speaking directly to you?
?
The king’s sign was so certain that no trick could cast doubt. Is your own conviction about the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him) built on this kind of unshakeable certainty, or does it waver with every intellectual challenge? What specific step will you take today to turn your inherited belief into necessary knowledge?
?
You have just read an analogy that makes the truth of prophethood as clear as a king’s public decree. Yet knowing this, are you still content to ignore the Prophet’s commands because they are inconvenient? Imagine yourself standing before that king: would you dare to disobey after he declared the messenger truthful? What is one command of the Prophet you have been neglecting, and what will you do about it before tomorrow?

Prophetic Character as Proof
Sanusi describes prophetic virtues as additional necessary proof of their truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impeccable character of the prophets, known through widely transmitted biographies, provides additional necessary proof of their truthfulness.

Core Idea
The prophets' moral perfection, known widely, leaves no doubt about their sincerity.

After the analogy of miracles, Sanusi presents a second, complementary proof: the known character and conduct of the prophets. Their biographies, transmitted widely (tawātur), reveal a consistent pattern of truthfulness, humility, compassion, and complete dedication to God. This moral perfection, combined with their endurance of severe hardship without any worldly benefit, provides an essential certainty that they are truthful messengers.

Guided Path
1
List the prophetic virtues
Go through the long list of virtues mentioned: truthfulness, renunciation of the world, humility, compassion, fear of God, perseverance, impartiality, forbearance.
These are not random but form a coherent moral profile.
2
Understand tawātur
Note that these qualities are known necessarily (ḍarūra) from their widely transmitted biographies (sīra). This makes the knowledge certain for those who receive the reports.
Tawātur ensures the information is reliable and beyond doubt.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why such a character cannot be fabricated. The prophets endured severe harm without worldly gain, proving their sincerity beyond question.
This complements the miracle proof, offering a holistic confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نَزَغْتُ ؟ بأن يخرق عادتهُ ، ويفعل كذا وكذا ممَّا ليسَ عادته أنْ يفعله ، ويخصني بالإجابة بذلك المصدقِ الفارقِ ، دونَ مَنْ يقومُ منكم يسأله مثل ذلكَ الخارقِ ، يبتغي به معارضتي ، وتكذيبي في مقالتي ؛ إذ ليس هو في الصِّدْقِ على مثل حالتي ۱ ثم قالَ : أَيُّها الملكُ ؛ إنْ كنتُ صادقاً فيما بلَّغْتُ عنكَ . . فاخرق عادتكَ ، وافعل كذا فأجابه الملكُ إلى ذلكَ ، وفعلَهُ على وَفْقِ ما سأَلَ وقد علم الجميعُ أَنَّهُ لا يُتوصَّلُ إلى مثل ذلكَ الفعلِ مِنَ الملكِ بحيلةٍ مِنَ الحيل . فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ ذلكَ الفعلَ مِنَ الملكِ يتنزَّلُ منزلة تصريحه بصِدْقِ الشخص في كلّ ما يُبلغ عنه ، والعلم بذلك ضروري لمَنْ حضرَ ذلكَ المجلس أو غابَ عنهُ ووصله خبره بالتواتر . ولا يخفى : أنَّ هذا المثالَ مطابق لحالِ الرُّسُلِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، ولا خفاءَ أَنَّهُ قد عُلِمَ ضرورةً مِنْ سيرتهم عليهم الصلاةُ السلامُ التزامُ الصِّدْقِ ، ورفع الهمَّةِ عن كل دناءة ، والزهد في الدنيا بأسرها ؛ بحيث استوى عندهم ذهبُها ومدرُها ، والتزامُ غـايـة الـتـواضـع مـعَ الفقراء والمساكين ، وإسقاط الجاه والمنزلة عندَ الخَلْقِ ، وطلبها عند الملكِ الحقِّ ، وعظيم ما جُبلوا عليهِ مِنَ الشفقة على المخلوقين ، والنصحُ التام لعبادِ اللهِ تعالى ، وكثرةُ جميع الخوفِ منه جلَّ وعزَّ ، والمبادرة لامتثالِ ما بَلَّغوا عنه قبل كلّ أحدٍ ، والمواظبة إلى المماتِ على دعاءِ الخَلْقِ إِلى اللهِ تعالى ، مع التسوية في ذلكَ بين وضيعهم ورفيعهم ، وغنيهم وفقيرهم ، وفطينهم وبليدهم ، وأعجمهم وفصيحهم، وحُرِّهم وعبدِهم، وذكرهم وأنثاهم . وحاضرهم وغائبهم ، وملكهم وسوقتِهم ثم سعة الصدور لحمل سوء إيذائهم وشدَّةِ جفائهم والرأفة على جميعِهم أكثرَ مِنْ رأفتِهم على أولادهم بل وعلى أنفسهم ، مِنْ غيرِ عوض يأخذونه منهم ، ولا منفعة دنيوية تحصل لهم مِنْ قِبَلِهم ، بل هم عليهم الصلاة والسلام تعرَّضوا بذلك لشدائد وأهوال نالتهم مِنْ جهتِهم ، لا يثبتُ لها إلا مَنْ هو على صميم الحقِّ ، قد شغلَهُ التلذذ برضا
And I did not [؟] that He should break His habit, and do such-and-such which is not His habit to do, and single me out for a response with that distinguishing sign, apart from whoever among you stands and asks Him for a similar miracle, seeking thereby to oppose me and deny my claim; for he is not in truthfulness in my state. Then he said: O king, if I am truthful in what I have conveyed from you, then break your habit and do such-and-such. So the king answered him and did it in accordance with what he asked. And everyone knew that such an action from the king cannot be achieved by any trick. So it is no secret that that action from the king takes the place of his explicit declaration of the person's truthfulness in everything he reports about him, and knowledge of that is necessary for whoever attended that assembly or was absent from it and whose report reached him via tawātur. Nor is it hidden that this example corresponds to the situation of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, and it is no secret that it is known necessarily from their biography, peace and blessings be upon them: their commitment to truthfulness, the elevation of their aspiration above every baseness, and their renunciation of the whole world such that its gold and dust were equal to them; their commitment to utmost humility with the poor and needy; their disregard for prestige and status among creatures, seeking them only with the true King; the great compassion with which they were disposed towards creatures; their sincere counsel to the servants of God Most High; their abundant fear of Him, Mighty and Majestic; their hastening to obey what they conveyed from Him before anyone else; their perseverance until death in calling creatures to God Most High, treating equally in that the low and the high, the rich and the poor, the intelligent and the dull, the non-Arab and the eloquent, the free and the slave, the male and the female, the present and the absent, the king and the commoner; then their forbearance in bearing the harm of their mistreatment and the severity of their cruelty; their compassion for all of them more than their compassion for their own children, even more than for themselves, without any recompense they take from them or any worldly benefit they obtain from them; rather, they, peace and blessings be upon them, exposed themselves thereby to hardships and terrors that befell them from them, which none endures except one who is upon solid truth, who is occupied by the delight of [God's] pleasure...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents a twofold proof of the truthfulness of prophets. First, it develops an analogy: a person claiming to speak on behalf of a king requests the king to perform an extraordinary act (khāriq al-ʿāda) as a sign of his truthfulness. The king complies in a way that no trickery can achieve. This act is equivalent to the king's explicit declaration that the person is truthful in all he reports. Knowledge of this is necessary (ḍarūrī) for those who witness it or receive tawātur reports. The analogy is then applied to the prophets: their miracles (muʿjizāt) serve the same function as the king's extraordinary act, providing direct divine confirmation of their truth. Second, the passage argues that the known character of the prophets—their truthfulness, disdain for worldly gain, humility, compassion, fear of God, perseverance in calling people to God despite harm, and their impartiality towards all people—is so impeccable that it necessarily (bi-ḍarūra) establishes their truthfulness. This character is known from their widely transmitted (mutawātir) biographies. The passage emphasizes that the prophets endured severe hardships without seeking any worldly benefit, proving their sincerity. The combination of miracles and moral perfection leaves no doubt about their prophethood.

Key Terms
سيرة
تواتر
ضرورة
خارق العادة

Correction: The passage emphasizes that the prophetic character is known necessarily (ḍarūra) through tawātur, making it objective and certain for all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a virtue of the prophets?
A) Wealth accumulation
B) Humility with the poor

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets treated every person equally, from the king to the commoner, without regard for status. In your daily interactions, do you find yourself showing favoritism towards those who can benefit you? What would it take for you to treat everyone with the same level of respect, regardless of their position?
?
The prophets endured severe harm and persecution from their people, yet they continued to call them to God with compassion. Think of someone who has wronged you or opposes your faith. Are you willing to continue showing them kindness and inviting them to truth, even if you receive nothing but hardship in return? What is holding you back?
?
The prophets renounced the entire world, making no distinction between gold and dust. Examine your own attachments: is there any worldly possession, status, or comfort that you would be unwilling to give up for the sake of God? If your faith required you to lose everything, would your character reflect the same sincerity as the prophets?
